arXiv:2602.07755v1 \[cs.AI\] 08 Feb 2026

# Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs

Report issue for preceding element

Yiming Xiong
Shengran Hu
Jeff Clune

Report issue for preceding element

###### Abstract

Report issue for preceding element

The statelessness of foundation models bottlenecks agentic systems’ ability to continually learn, a core capability for long-horizon reasoning and adaptation. To address this limitation, agentic systems commonly incorporate memory modules to retain and reuse past experience, aiming for continual learning during test time. However, most existing memory designs are human-crafted and fixed, which limits their ability to adapt to the diversity and non-stationarity of real-world tasks. In this paper, we introduce ALMA (Automated meta-Learning of Memory designs for Agentic systems), a framework that meta-learns memory designs to replace hand-engineered memory designs, therefore minimizing human effort and enabling agentic systems to be continual learners across diverse domains. Our approach employs a Meta Agent that searches over memory designs expressed as executable code in an open-ended manner, theoretically allowing the discovery of arbitrary memory designs, including database schemas as well as their retrieval and update mechanisms. Extensive experiments across four sequential decision-making domains demonstrate that the learned memory designs enable more effective and efficient learning from experience than state-of-the-art human-crafted memory designs on all benchmarks. When developed and deployed safely, ALMA represents a step toward self-improving AI systems that learn to be adaptive, continual learners.
All code is open-sourced at [https://github.com/zksha/alma.git](https://github.com/zksha/alma.git "").

Report issue for preceding element

Machine Learning, ICML, Meta-Learning, Agentic System, Continual Learning, Agentic Memory

## 1 Introduction

Report issue for preceding element

Agentic systems powered by Foundation Models (FMs) have enabled autonomous decision-making and task execution across a wide range of domains (Yao et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib1 "ReAct: synergizing reasoning and acting in language models"); Wang et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib2 "Voyager: an open-ended embodied agent with large language models"); Yang et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib3 "SWE-agent: agent-computer interfaces enable automated software engineering")). However, the stateless nature of FMs during inference challenges agentic systems to accumulate experience and continually learn from past interactions, causing them to repeatedly solve tasks from scratch and limiting their ability to improve over time (Child et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib5 "Generating long sequences with sparse transformers"); Bulatov et al., [2022](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib4 "Recurrent memory transformer")). Memory addresses this limitation by enabling agentic systems to continually store and reuse past experiences (Zhong et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib6 "MemoryBank: enhancing large language models with long-term memory"); Packer et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib7 "MemGPT: towards llms as operating systems")). This allows accumulated experience to inform decision-making in future tasks, aiming for continual learning over time.

Report issue for preceding element

However, memory design, the architectural specification that determines how memories are represented, stored, retrieved, and updated, is still predominantly handcrafted by humans. Diverse domains require distinct memory designs to leverage unique aspects of experience, resulting in human researchers manually tailoring a wide array of designs to specific tasks (Zhang et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib9 "A survey on the memory mechanism of large language model based agents"); Hu et al., [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib8 "Memory in the age of ai agents")).
For example, in conversational agents, memory may focus on retaining facts about the user, such as preferences and personal information (Chhikara et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib10 "Mem0: building production-ready ai agents with scalable long-term memory"); Rasmussen et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib11 "Zep: a temporal knowledge graph architecture for agent memory")). In contrast, for strategic games, memory should extract abstract skills and strategies from previous interactions rather than details that may change across episodes (Tang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib75 "AGENT KB: a hierarchical memory framework for cross-domain agentic problem solving"); Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory")).
Therefore, manually identifying an optimal memory design for each domain is both difficult and labor-intensive.

Report issue for preceding element

A recurring theme in machine learning history is that handcrafted components in AI systems are eventually replaced by learned, more effective ones (Clune, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib13 "AI-gas: ai-generating algorithms, an alternate paradigm for producing general artificial intelligence"); Hutter et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib14 "Automated machine learning - methods, systems, challenges"); Sutton, [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib15 "The bitter lesson")). A classic example of this paradigm is the transition from hand-designed features to learned representations in computer vision (Krizhevsky et al., [2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib16 "ImageNet classification with deep convolutional neural networks")). More recently, the trend of “learning to learn” has expanded to learning neural architectures (Zoph and Le, [2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib17 "Neural architecture search with reinforcement learning")), optimization algorithms (Andrychowicz et al., [2016](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib18 "Learning to learn by gradient descent by gradient descent")), training environments (Wang et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib38 "POET: open-ended coevolution of environments and their optimized solutions"); Zhang et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib91 "OMNI: open-endedness via models of human notions of interestingness"); Faldor et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib49 "OMNI-epic: open-endedness via models of human notions of interestingness with environments programmed in code")), and designs for agentic systems (Hu et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib19 "Automated design of agentic systems"); Zhang et al., [2025e](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib48 "Darwin godel machine: open-ended evolution of self-improving agents")), among others. In this paper, we investigate whether agentic systems can learn to continually learn by automating the design of their memory components. This enables memory designs to be specialized for diverse domains without relying on manual engineering.

Report issue for preceding element

![Refer to caption](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1/x1.png)

Report issue for preceding element

Figure 1: Open-ended Exploration Process of ALMA.
The Meta Agent first ideates and proposes a plan by reflecting on the code and evaluation logs of the sampled memory design. It then implements the plan by programming the new design in code. Finally, it verifies the correctness of the new memory design and evaluates it with an agentic system. The evaluated memory design is subsequently added to the memory design archive for future sampling.
Report issue for preceding element

We propose ALMA (Automated meta-Learning of Memory designs for Agentic systems), a framework that adopts a Meta Agent to explore novel memory designs for agentic systems through open-ended exploration.
We use code as the search space, giving ALMA the theoretical potential to build any memory design.
As shown in [Figure1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S1.F1 "In 1 Introduction ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs"), the Meta Agent samples previously explored memory designs from an archive, where all explored memory designs and their evaluation logs are stored.
Then the Meta Agent reflects on the sampled memory designs to generate new ideas and plans, which are implemented in code.
The new designs are subsequently validated and evaluated, with the resulting logs added back to the archive to guide future sampling.

Report issue for preceding element

We evaluate our method across four sequential decision-making domains, including ALFWorld (Shridhar et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib21 "{alfw}orld: aligning text and embodied environments for interactive learning")), TextWorld (Côté et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib23 "TextWorld: a learning environment for text-based games")), Baba Is AI (Cloos et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib24 "Baba is ai: break the rules to beat the benchmark")), and MiniHack (Samvelyan et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib25 "MiniHack the planet: a sandbox for open-ended reinforcement learning research")). These domains serve as testbeds for evaluating an agent’s capability to continually learn from experience. In these domains, agents benefit from storing and reusing their experiences to acquire effective strategies and useful information that is absent from the FMs’ pre-trained knowledge. The results show that ALMA can discover memory designs that are tailored for the needs of different domains, consistently surpassing state-of-the-art human-designed memory baselines ( [Table1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.T1 "In 4.1 Experiment Setup ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")). Beyond superior performance, our learned memory designs are more cost-efficient than most human-designed memory baselines ( [Figure6](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F6 "In 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")). Furthermore, the learned memory designs scale performance more effectively with memory size and learn faster when facing task distribution shifts ( [Figures4](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F4 "In 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs") and [5](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F5 "Figure 5 ‣ 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")). Overall, the memory designs learned by ALMA demonstrate a superior ability to help agentic systems continually learn, paving the way towards developing lifelong learning agents in dynamic environments.

Report issue for preceding element

## 2 Related Work

Report issue for preceding element

#### Memory for Agentic Systems.

Report issue for preceding element

Memory enables agentic systems to continually learn despite the statelessness of FMs (Hu et al., [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib8 "Memory in the age of ai agents"); Zhang et al., [2025i](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib66 "A survey on the memory mechanism of large language model-based agents")). Existing work has categorized memory mechanisms into token-level memory, parametric memory, and latent memory (Hu et al., [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib8 "Memory in the age of ai agents")). Token-level memory defines databases and update rules to store information and extract insights from interaction trajectories between agents and environments. When addressing a new task, the system retrieves relevant experiences via defined procedures, which are then appended to the prompts of agentic systems as context (Chhikara et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib10 "Mem0: building production-ready ai agents with scalable long-term memory"); Rasmussen et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib11 "Zep: a temporal knowledge graph architecture for agent memory"); Nan et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib26 "Nemori: self-organizing agent memory inspired by cognitive science"); Zhang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib27 "G-memory: tracing hierarchical memory for multi-agent systems"); Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory"); Suzgun et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib46 "Dynamic cheatsheet: test-time learning with adaptive memory"); Zhao et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib83 "ExpeL: llm agents are experiential learners")). For example, G-Memory (Zhang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib27 "G-memory: tracing hierarchical memory for multi-agent systems")) extracts experiences and stores them in a graph-based database, and retrieves relevant experiences for new tasks via graph traversal. Reasoning Bank (Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory")) performs task-wise experience generation during memory updates, while Dynamic Cheatsheet (Suzgun et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib46 "Dynamic cheatsheet: test-time learning with adaptive memory")) includes accumulation of insights across trajectories. Current works also explore training models to select from predefined operations for storing and reusing experience in memory (Zhou et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib84 "Memento: fine-tuning llm agents without fine-tuning llms"); Yan et al., [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib85 "Memory-r1: enhancing large language model agents to manage and utilize memories via reinforcement learning"); Liang et al., [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib86 "Learning how to remember: a meta-cognitive management method for structured and transferable agent memory"); Wang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib87 "Mem-α: learning memory construction via reinforcement learning")). Other categories of memory include parametric and latent memory, which encode experience implicitly within the model’s weights (Qin et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib47 "Various lengths, constant speed: efficient language modeling with lightning attention"); Zhang et al., [2025g](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib30 "Agent learning via early experience")) or latent hidden states (Wang et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib29 "MEMORYLLM: towards self-updatable large language models"); Zhang et al., [2025b](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib28 "MemGen: weaving generative latent memory for self-evolving agents"); Zou et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib89 "Latent collaboration in multi-agent systems")) rather than relying on explicit text-based retrieval. The “Learning to learn” paradigm can theoretically support the learning of all categories of memory. In this paper, we focus on token-level memory, as it allows for faster evaluation without additional model training and provides more interpretable, transferable memory representations.

Report issue for preceding element

#### AI-generating algorithms and the “Learning to learn” paradigm.

Report issue for preceding element

AI-generating algorithms (Clune, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib13 "AI-gas: ai-generating algorithms, an alternate paradigm for producing general artificial intelligence")) and automated machine learning (Hutter et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib14 "Automated machine learning - methods, systems, challenges")) aim to replace hand-engineered components with automatically learned ones. The approach has three key pillars: (1) meta-learning architectures, (2) meta-learning learning algorithms, and (3) the automatic generation of learning environments (Clune, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib13 "AI-gas: ai-generating algorithms, an alternate paradigm for producing general artificial intelligence")).
Neural Architecture Search (Elsken et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib32 "Neural architecture search: a survey"); Lu et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib33 "NSGA-net: neural architecture search using multi-objective genetic algorithm"); Hu et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib31 "Accelerating multi-objective neural architecture search by random-weight evaluation")) primarily contributes to the first pillar by automating the discovery of neural architectures. Research in MAML (Finn et al., [2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib34 "Model-agnostic meta-learning for fast adaptation of deep networks")), Meta-RL (Wang et al., [2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib36 "Learning to reinforcement learn"); Duan et al., [2016](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib35 "RL2: fast reinforcement learning via slow reinforcement learning"); Norman and Clune, [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib37 "First-explore, then exploit: meta-learning to solve hard exploration-exploitation trade-offs")), and recent Automated Algorithm Design (Liu et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib51 "Evolution of heuristics: towards efficient automatic algorithm design using large language model"), [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib65 "A systematic survey on large language models for algorithm design")) has explored the second pillar by focusing on “Learning to learn”, developing algorithms that enable agents to adapt their learning behavior across tasks (Beaulieu et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib78 "Learning to continually learn"); Lu et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib53 "Discovering preference optimization algorithms with and for large language models")).
The third pillar includes approaches such as POET (Wang et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib38 "POET: open-ended coevolution of environments and their optimized solutions"), [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib40 "Enhanced POET: open-ended reinforcement learning through unbounded invention of learning challenges and their solutions")), as well as more recent work on dynamic environment generation with agentic systems (Aki et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib41 "LLM-poet: evolving complex environments using large language models"); Guo et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib42 "GenEnv: difficulty-aligned co-evolution between llm agents and environment simulators"); Zhang et al., [2024a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib91 "OMNI: open-endedness via models of human notions of interestingness")).
Our work is more related to the second pillar, as ALMA learns how memory designs can better enable agentic systems to continually learn. Furthermore, recent AI-generating algorithms and automated machine learning approaches have leveraged FMs to explore new components in AI systems through code generation (Hu et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib19 "Automated design of agentic systems"); Zhang et al., [2025e](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib48 "Darwin godel machine: open-ended evolution of self-improving agents"); Faldor et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib49 "OMNI-epic: open-endedness via models of human notions of interestingness with environments programmed in code"); Yamada et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib50 "The ai scientist-v2: workshop-level automated scientific discovery via agentic tree search"); Lu et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib52 "The ai scientist: towards fully automated open-ended scientific discovery"), [a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib53 "Discovering preference optimization algorithms with and for large language models"), [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib54 "Automated capability discovery via foundation model self-exploration"); Lehman et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib55 "Evolution through large models")).
Similarly, we adopt a Meta Agent powered by FMs to propose novel memory designs by programming in code.

Report issue for preceding element

#### Automated Design of Agentic Systems.

Report issue for preceding element

Previous work has studied the automated design of agentic systems to learn better components in agentic systems (Hu et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib19 "Automated design of agentic systems"); Zhang et al., [2025f](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib56 "AFlow: automating agentic workflow generation"); Zhou et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib57 "Symbolic learning enables self-evolving agents"); Yin et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib58 "Gödel agent: a self-referential agent framework for recursively self-improvement"); Zhuge et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib59 "GPTSwarm: language agents as optimizable graphs"); Rosser and Foerster, [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib60 "AgentBreeder: mitigating the ai safety impact of multi-agent scaffolds via self-improvement"); Zhang et al., [2025c](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib61 "Multi-agent architecture search via agentic supernet"); Shang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib43 "AgentSquare: automatic llm agent search in modular design space"); Zhang et al., [2025h](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib44 "SwarmAgentic: towards fully automated agentic system generation via swarm intelligence"), [c](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib61 "Multi-agent architecture search via agentic supernet"); Ye et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib62 "MAS-GPT: training LLMs to build LLM-based multi-agent systems"); Zhang et al., [2025e](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib48 "Darwin godel machine: open-ended evolution of self-improving agents")). Since recent works such as ADAS (Hu et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib19 "Automated design of agentic systems")) employ code as the search space for agentic system designs, they theoretically enable the learning of the memory component. For example, AgentSquare (Shang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib43 "AgentSquare: automatic llm agent search in modular design space")) attempts to co-learn memory designs along with other components like tools in agentic systems.
However, while existing works evaluate only the one-shot performance of agentic systems, ALMA explicitly optimizes for a memory design’s capability to facilitate continual learning from past experience, enabling the discovery of more effective memory designs.
A concurrent work to ALMA also explores an approach for learning memory designs (Zhang et al., [2025d](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib45 "MemEvolve: meta-evolution of agent memory systems")). However, it relies on the initialization with many existing handcrafted memory designs and greedy selection of top-performing designs, which limits open-ended exploration.
In contrast, ALMA adopts an open-ended exploration of memory designs and learns from scratch. Open-ended exploration is shown to be important to search for high-performance structure (Lehman and Stanley, [2008](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib92 "Exploiting open-endedness to solve problems through the search for novelty"), [2011a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib93 "Abandoning objectives: evolution through the search for novelty alone"), [2011b](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib95 "Evolving a diversity of virtual creatures through novelty search and local competition"); Conti et al., [2018](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib96 "Improving exploration in evolution strategies for deep reinforcement learning via a population of novelty-seeking agents"); Stanley, [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib97 "Why open-endedness matters")). Therefore, incorporating open-ended exploration into the search for optimal memory designs presents a promising avenue. Our ablation studies show that indeed open-ended exploration can learn better memory designs than greedy-selection-based optimization (Appendix [C.2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A3.SS2 "C.2 Results of Greedy Exploration ‣ Appendix C Result Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")).

Report issue for preceding element

## 3 Learning of Memory Designs

Report issue for preceding element

We propose ALMA, a framework for learning memory designs within a code-based search space via open-ended exploration, discovering memory designs that effectively collect, summarize, and reuse experience.
Specifically, a Meta Agent (Hu et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib19 "Automated design of agentic systems")) iteratively proposes a new candidate memory design by reflecting on the results of previously discovered designs, implements the new design through code generation and debugging, and evaluates its performance by integrating it into an agentic system.
The pseudocode of our learning process is provided in Appendix [A.6](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A1.SS6 "A.6 Pseudocode ‣ Appendix A Algorithmic Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

### 3.1 Search Space for Memory Designs

Report issue for preceding element

The search space defines all memory designs that can be represented and discovered. In this paper, we use code as the search space, thus theoretically allowing all possible memory designs to be discovered due to the Turing completeness of many programming languages, i.e., Python in our case.
Representing memory designs in code also enables interpretability and allows FMs to leverage their prior knowledge acquired during pretraining about agentic systems and coding (Hu et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib19 "Automated design of agentic systems")).
Due to the vastness of the code-based search space, it is inefficient for the Meta Agent to construct all basic functions of a memory design from scratch.
We therefore provide the Meta Agent a simple abstraction that facilitates exploration while preserving flexibility.
Our abstraction design is inspired by common patterns observed in existing handcrafted memory designs (Nan et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib26 "Nemori: self-organizing agent memory inspired by cognitive science"); Chhikara et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib10 "Mem0: building production-ready ai agents with scalable long-term memory"); Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory"); Zhang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib27 "G-memory: tracing hierarchical memory for multi-agent systems"); Tang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib75 "AGENT KB: a hierarchical memory framework for cross-domain agentic problem solving"); Suzgun et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib46 "Dynamic cheatsheet: test-time learning with adaptive memory")). The abstraction allows the memory module to interact with the agentic system through two primary interfaces: general\_update() and general\_retrieve(). After collecting new interactions with the environment, the agent calls general\_update() to extract useful experience into memory. When faced with a new task, the agent calls general\_retrieve() to access relevant experience.
Internally, each interface can coordinate multiple sub-modules.
Each sub-module implements its update() and retrieve() logic,
and optionally maintains its own database when needed. The output of one sub-module can serve as input for subsequent sub-modules, enabling hierarchical and modular memory designs.
For example, ReasoningBank (Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory")) employs a vector-database sub-module to store task descriptions, along with an additional sub-module that extracts experience learned from individual tasks with FMs.
Similarly, G-memory (Zhang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib27 "G-memory: tracing hierarchical memory for multi-agent systems")) incorporates a graph-database sub-module to model linkages between extracted insights and a vector-database sub-module to store task descriptions.
We implement the described abstraction in Python Abstract Classes, and the code is available in Appendix [A.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A1.SS1 "A.1 Search Space ‣ Appendix A Algorithmic Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

### 3.2 Evaluation of Memory Designs

Report issue for preceding element

Based on existing work (Nan et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib26 "Nemori: self-organizing agent memory inspired by cognitive science"); Chhikara et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib10 "Mem0: building production-ready ai agents with scalable long-term memory"); Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory"); Zhang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib27 "G-memory: tracing hierarchical memory for multi-agent systems"); Tang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib75 "AGENT KB: a hierarchical memory framework for cross-domain agentic problem solving"); Suzgun et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib46 "Dynamic cheatsheet: test-time learning with adaptive memory"); Hu et al., [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib8 "Memory in the age of ai agents"); Wang et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib73 "Agent workflow memory"); Zhong et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib6 "MemoryBank: enhancing large language models with long-term memory"); Packer et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib7 "MemGPT: towards llms as operating systems"); Fang et al., [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib74 "Memp: exploring agent procedural memory"); Zhao et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib83 "ExpeL: llm agents are experiential learners")), we summarize that memory components in agentic systems generally operate in two phases.

Report issue for preceding element

Memory Collection Phase.
The goal of this phase is not task success, but rather collecting knowledge and updating memory for later use in the Deployment Phase. There are two common ways to obtain raw trajectories for updating memory at this stage: (1) leveraging existing agent trajectories from available datasets (Zhong et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib6 "MemoryBank: enhancing large language models with long-term memory"); Chhikara et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib10 "Mem0: building production-ready ai agents with scalable long-term memory")), or (2) starting from an empty memory state and collecting trajectories by running the agent on tasks (Fang et al., [2026](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib74 "Memp: exploring agent procedural memory"); Tang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib75 "AGENT KB: a hierarchical memory framework for cross-domain agentic problem solving")). This phase typically does not involve retrieving from memory (Zhong et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib6 "MemoryBank: enhancing large language models with long-term memory"); Chhikara et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib10 "Mem0: building production-ready ai agents with scalable long-term memory"); Wang et al., [2024b](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib73 "Agent workflow memory"); Packer et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib7 "MemGPT: towards llms as operating systems"); Rasmussen et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib11 "Zep: a temporal knowledge graph architecture for agent memory")).

Report issue for preceding element

Deployment Phase.
This is the main stage where the agentic system is deployed to applications and expected to achieve task success. Given the memory collected after the Memory Collection Phase, the agentic system retrieves from memory for each incoming task and attempts to solve it. This stage can operate in two modes: dynamic, which updates memory with newly collected trajectories from incoming tasks (Zhang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib27 "G-memory: tracing hierarchical memory for multi-agent systems"); Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory")), or static, which keeps memory fixed (Tang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib75 "AGENT KB: a hierarchical memory framework for cross-domain agentic problem solving"); Rasmussen et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib11 "Zep: a temporal knowledge graph architecture for agent memory")).

Report issue for preceding element

We evaluate each memory design by first running the Memory Collection Phase and then recording the success rate during the Deployment Phase, using an identical, fixed agentic system, with the memory design being evaluated. The two modes in the Deployment Phase serve different purposes: static mode assesses how effectively the agent leverages a fixed memory to solve new tasks, while dynamic mode measures how well a memory design adapts to a new task distribution through dynamic updates and retrieval. When learning memory designs, we use only static mode to reduce variance during evaluations. The general\_update and general\_retrieve modules contribute to the performance of the memory design through memory collection and knowledge retrieval, respectively. When testing the best-learned memory design, we evaluate under both modes.
More details on evaluations are available in Appendix [A.2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A1.SS2 "A.2 Evaluation of Memory design ‣ Appendix A Algorithmic Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

### 3.3 Open-Ended Exploration

Report issue for preceding element

Similar to ADAS (Hu et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib19 "Automated design of agentic systems")), we propose a framework for open-ended exploration of memory designs with a Meta Agent programming novel memory designs in code.
The algorithm proceeds as follows: (1) An archive is initialized with Python abstract classes as an empty memory design template. (2) The Meta Agent samples previously discovered designs from the archive, reflects on their performance outcomes, and proposes new memory designs. (3) The newly proposed designs are evaluated on the target domain. If errors occur during evaluation, the Meta Agent performs self-reflection to refine the design, repeating this process up to three times if necessary. (4) Finally, the memory design is added to the archive along with its evaluation results and logs, and the process continues with the updated archive until the maximum number of iterations is reached.

Report issue for preceding element

Sampling Memory Designs.
We sample memory designs from the archive to serve as stepping stones for the exploration of new memory designs at each step.
Similar to DGM (Zhang et al., [2025e](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib48 "Darwin godel machine: open-ended evolution of self-improving agents")), each design in the archive is assigned a sampling probability roughly proportional to its success rate and inversely proportional to the number of times it is sampled. This prioritizes designs that perform well but have been sampled less frequently, enabling a balance between refining successful designs and exploring underexplored candidates. All designs maintain non-zero sampling probabilities, keeping all potential improvements reachable.
Details are provided in Appendix [A.4](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A1.SS4 "A.4 Memory Design Archive and Sampling Process ‣ Appendix A Algorithmic Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

Proposing New Memory Design by the Meta Agent.
After sampling previous memory designs from the archive, the Meta Agent reflects on their evaluation results and proposes new memory designs by implementing them in code.
Specifically, the Meta Agent first proposes ideas, then plans for the new designs through analyzing the sampled designs along with their success rate and interaction logs.
Based on the plans, the Meta Agent implements the new memory designs in code and performs trial runs. Runtime errors during the trial runs trigger debugging reflections to refine the implementations.
Finally, the new memory designs are evaluated. Their evaluation logs are added to the memory design archive to support future sampling.
Prompts for planning, implementation, and debugging are in Appendix [A.5](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A1.SS5 "A.5 Prompts of Meta Agent ‣ Appendix A Algorithmic Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

## 4 Experiments

Report issue for preceding element

### 4.1 Experiment Setup

Report issue for preceding element

We perform ALMA on four sequential decision-making benchmarks (Section [4.2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.SS2 "4.2 Benchmarks ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")).
For each benchmark, the dataset is divided into a learning set and a testing set, which are used for memory design learning and for the testing of the learned memory designs to ensure testing on unseen data.
Both sets are further split evenly, with the first half used for the Memory Collection Phase and the second half for the Deployment Phase ( [Section3.2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S3.SS2 "3.2 Evaluation of Memory Designs ‣ 3 Learning of Memory Designs ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")). To reduce variance, we execute the Deployment Phase three times for learning and testing, and report the average success rate with the standard error.
For clarity, in the following sections, “success rate” refers to the average success rate over three runs.

Report issue for preceding element

At each learning step, we sample up to five memory designs from the archive without replacement. We expect the choice between sampling with or without replacement to have minimal impact (Appendix [B.3](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A2.SS3 "B.3 Additional Details of Learning Process ‣ Appendix B Experiment Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")).
We run ALMA for 11 learning steps to discover 43 memory designs.

Report issue for preceding element

During the learning of memory designs, GPT-5 is used in the Meta Agent, while GPT-5-nano is used in the fixed agentic system for evaluating memory designs. While the Meta Agent can choose to use any model (or train models) as FMs within memory designs, we provide GPT-4o-mini, GPT-4.1, and text-embedding-3-small as tools for the Meta Agent to explore workflows such as extracting insights or computing semantic similarity. More details on the learning process setup are available in Appendix [B.3](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A2.SS3 "B.3 Additional Details of Learning Process ‣ Appendix B Experiment Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

During the testing of the learned memory designs, we test under two agentic system settings: the first employs GPT-5-nano, matching the learning setup. The second replaces it with a more capable FM, GPT-5-mini, to assess the transferability of memory designs to stronger agentic systems ( [Table1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.T1 "In 4.1 Experiment Setup ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")). We use GPT-4o-mini and text-embedding-3-small as FMs across all memory designs during testing to ensure a fair comparison. More details on the testing setup are available in Appendix [B.4](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A2.SS4 "B.4 Additional Details of Testing ‣ Appendix B Experiment Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

Table 1: Comparison of success rates between the learned memory designs and baseline memory designs across environments.Top: Learning and testing the memory designs with GPT-5-nano as the FM in the agentic system. Bottom: Transferring the memory designs to test with GPT-5-mini in the agentic system. In both cases, learned memory designs outperform human-designed baselines, demonstrating that they are more effective than human-designed memory and generalize robustly across different FMs.
Results are reported as mean ±\\pm standard error in percentages, calculated over three runs of the Deployment Phase. The relative gains over the no-memory baseline are color-coded.

Report issue for preceding element

### 4.2 Benchmarks

Report issue for preceding element

We adopt four sequential decision-making benchmarks with varying levels of difficulty (Paglieri et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib63 "BALROG: benchmarking agentic llm and vlm reasoning on games")).
In all benchmarks, agentic systems perceive the environment through text and interact with it by generating actions in natural language.
The benchmarks are well-suited for evaluating memory designs because they require acquiring and utilizing experience that does not exist in the pre-trained knowledge of FMs (Paglieri et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib63 "BALROG: benchmarking agentic llm and vlm reasoning on games")).
We test ALMA on four benchmarks:
(1) ALFWorld (Shridhar et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib21 "{alfw}orld: aligning text and embodied environments for interactive learning")), a text-based simulation of embodied household tasks that challenges agents to ground language instructions to action sequences in kitchen environments; (2) TextWorld (Côté et al., [2019](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib23 "TextWorld: a learning environment for text-based games")), text adventure games that require systematic exploration and reasoning to navigate partially observable environments; (3) Baba Is AI (Cloos et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib24 "Baba is ai: break the rules to beat the benchmark")), a strategic puzzle game where agents must manipulate the rules of the game, requiring complex reasoning and adaptation to changing game mechanics; (4) MiniHack (Samvelyan et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib25 "MiniHack the planet: a sandbox for open-ended reinforcement learning research")), a simplified version of NetHack (Küttler et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib77 "The nethack learning environment")) that challenges agents with long-horizon decision-making in procedurally generated dungeon environments.

Report issue for preceding element

For ALFWorld, we are using the default setting (Shridhar et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib21 "{alfw}orld: aligning text and embodied environments for interactive learning")). For all other benchmarks, we use BALROG (Paglieri et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib63 "BALROG: benchmarking agentic llm and vlm reasoning on games")), following its settings and prompt for interactions during learning and final testing. More details on benchmarks are available in Appendix [B.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A2.SS1 "B.1 Benchmark Details ‣ Appendix B Experiment Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

### 4.3 Baselines

Report issue for preceding element

We select four representative state-of-the-art human-designed memory systems as baselines. These baselines are entirely handcrafted, with all aspects of the design (e.g., what to store, how to update, and how to retrieve) manually specified and inserted retrieved knowledge into the context of agentic systems, representing various mainstream approaches in prior work. More details are available in Appendix [B.2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A2.SS2 "B.2 Baseline Details ‣ Appendix B Experiment Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

Trajectory Retrieval. A straightforward but strong baseline that retrieves similar trajectories (Park et al., [2023](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib81 "Generative agents: interactive simulacra of human behavior"); Xu et al., [2024](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib80 "Retrieval meets long context large language models")). Task descriptions and trajectories are stored during memory update. During memory retrieval, the most relevant past trajectory is retrieved as knowledge based on the similarity of the task description embedding.

Report issue for preceding element

ReasoningBank. The memory designs (Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory")) organize and extract experience on a per-task basis, enabling the retrieval of experience relevant to each task. During memory update, experiences are extracted from each trajectory by FMs and stored together with the corresponding task description. The experiences corresponding to the most similar task description are retrieved as knowledge.

Report issue for preceding element

Dynamic Cheatsheet (Cumulative). A global semantic memory incrementally accumulates experience by FMs across all past trajectories (Suzgun et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib46 "Dynamic cheatsheet: test-time learning with adaptive memory")). The trajectories are sequentially used to update a global cheatsheet, which works as knowledge across all tasks.

Report issue for preceding element

G-memory. A hierarchical graph-based memory design (Zhang et al., [2025a](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib27 "G-memory: tracing hierarchical memory for multi-agent systems")). During the update of memory, it builds a hierarchical memory where insights and key steps in trajectories are connected in a graph based on task descriptions. When facing new tasks, it traverses the graph to retrieve relevant insights and key steps as knowledge.

Report issue for preceding element

### 4.4 Results

Report issue for preceding element

![Refer to caption](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1/x2.png)

Report issue for preceding element

Figure 2: The learning process of ALMA on Baba Is AI, using GPT-5-nano as the FM in an agentic system. The learning processes of other benchmarks are shown in Appendix [C.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A3.SS1 "C.1 Learning Processes on Other Benchmarks ‣ Appendix C Result Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").
Left: The memory design archive tree, where each node represents a memory design produced during the open-ended exploration for ever-better memory designs. Node colors indicate the success rate, and edges indicate that each child node is derived from its parent. The memory design with the highest success rate is used as the final learned memory design.
Right: The step-wise learning progress. ALMA progressively discovers memory designs by building on an ever-growing archive of previous discoveries. The path from the root memory design to the best memory design highlights the importance of open-ended exploration, where designs with moderate success rates serve as stepping stones toward optimal solutions.
Report issue for preceding element

For each benchmark, we select the memory design achieving the highest success rate during the learning of memory designs as the best-learned memory design. We then evaluate both human-designed baselines and the best-learned memory designs using the testing process described in [Section4.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.SS1 "4.1 Experiment Setup ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs") with static mode in the Deployment Phase ( [Section3.2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S3.SS2 "3.2 Evaluation of Memory Designs ‣ 3 Learning of Memory Designs ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")).

Report issue for preceding element

As shown in [Table1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.T1 "In 4.1 Experiment Setup ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs") Top, with the agentic system powered by GPT-5-nano, the learned memory designs achieve an overall improvement of 6.2% compared to the no-memory baseline, and outperform all state-of-the-art human-designed memory baselines.
These improvements are consistent across benchmarks, demonstrating that learned memory designs can more effectively store and reuse past experience to continually learn than manual designs.

Report issue for preceding element

We further evaluate learned memory designs by changing the FM in the agentic system from GPT-5-nano to GPT-5-mini ( [Table1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.T1 "In 4.1 Experiment Setup ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs") Bottom).
The learned memory designs improve the overall average success rate by 12.8% over the no-memory baseline and outperform all human-designed baselines. These results demonstrate that the discovered memory designs generalize across different FMs, indicating that the improvements are robust and not tied to a specific model. The larger improvement observed with more capable FMs (12.8% vs 6.2%) yields a delta of 6.6%. This delta exceeds those of all human-designed memory baselines, suggesting that our learned memory designs provide stronger support to more capable agentic systems.

Report issue for preceding element

To provide a closer look at the learning process, we show the learning process on Baba Is AI and the tree visualization of the resulting memory design archive ( [Figure2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F2 "In 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")).
The Meta Agent explores diverse new designs on the path toward the best-learned memory design, building upon designs with moderate success rates that nevertheless have the potential to evolve into the optimal memory design. As shown in [Figure2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F2 "In 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs"), during open-ended exploration, the Meta Agent incrementally introduces mechanisms like property validation and spatial object normalization.
While these mechanisms may not yield immediate performance gains, they serve as stepping stones that contribute to the best-learned memory design when key mechanisms (e.g., strategy switching) are introduced.
Analyses and visualizations of the learning processes for the other benchmarks are available in the Appendix  [C.1](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A3.SS1 "C.1 Learning Processes on Other Benchmarks ‣ Appendix C Result Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").

Report issue for preceding element

To evaluate the effectiveness of the open-ended exploration in ALMA, we conduct an ablation study comparing against greedy search, which always samples the design with the highest success rate to propose new designs. More details on greedy search are available in Appendix [B.3](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A2.SS3 "B.3 Additional Details of Learning Process ‣ Appendix B Experiment Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs"). We perform the learning process with greedy search on ALFWorld, resulting in success rates of 11.9% and 77.1% when testing the best-learned memory design with the agentic system powered by GPT-5-nano and GPT-5-mini, respectively (Appendix [C.2](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A3.SS2 "C.2 Results of Greedy Exploration ‣ Appendix C Result Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")). In both cases (for GPT-5-nano, 11.9% vs 12.4%; for GPT-5-mini, 77.1% vs 87.1%), success rates are lower than those achieved by designs learned with open-ended exploration, demonstrating the benefits of an exploration-driven learning process over greedy search.

Report issue for preceding element

![Refer to caption](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1/x3.png)

Report issue for preceding elementFigure 3: The visualization of the best-learned memory designs across different benchmarks. Each sub-module in memory designs may have a dedicated database or none, depending on its function, and arrows show the retrieval and update workflows in memory designs. The name and explanation of each sub-module are generated by Meta Agent and manually summarized, respectively. Example code and output for a learned memory design are provided.
Report issue for preceding element

We visualize the learned memory designs for each domain ( [Figure3](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F3 "In 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")), showing that ALMA discovers effective memory structures adapted to diverse task requirements. For games with explicit object interaction goals (e.g., ALFWorld and TextWorld), the learned memory designs tend to store fine-grained knowledge, such as spatial relationships between objects and room layouts. In contrast, for tasks that require more complex reasoning (e.g., Baba Is AI and MiniHack), the memory designs favor domain-specific abstract strategy, including strategy libraries and plan synthesis. This pattern indicates that ALMA automatically specializes memory designs to the demands of each domain.

Report issue for preceding element

![Refer to caption](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1/x4.png)

Report issue for preceding elementFigure 4: Success rates of different memory designs in ALFWorld as the task size during the memory collection phase increases. Evaluations are performed using static mode during the Deployment Phase to study how performance scales with collected static memory. GPT-5-mini is used as the FM in the agentic system during testing. Shaded areas indicate standard error, calculated over three runs of the Deployment Phase. The learned memory design achieves higher performance faster with limited data and scales better than human-designed baselines.
Report issue for preceding element

To study how performance scales with experience, we evaluate memory designs using different sizes of tasks during the Memory Collection Phase and using static mode during the Deployment Phase.
As shown in [Figure4](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F4 "In 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs"), compared to manual memory designs, the learned memory design achieves higher performance faster with limited data and scales better as more trajectories are provided, demonstrating both higher sample efficiency and stronger scalability.
We observe that increasing the trajectory size does not necessarily lead to a monotonic improvement in success rate, which is expected since trajectories may vary in quality (Ouyang et al., [2025](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib12 "ReasoningBank: scaling agent self-evolving with reasoning memory")).

Report issue for preceding element

![Refer to caption](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1/x5.png)

Report issue for preceding element

Figure 5: Success rates of different memory designs in ALFWorld under task distribution shift in the Deployment Phase. Memory is collected from tasks in valid\_seen during the Memory Collection Phase and evaluated on the valid\_unseen dataset with dynamic mode during the Deployment Phase, using GPT-5-mini as the FM in the agentic system. The error bars indicate the standard errors calculated over three runs of the Deployment Phase. The learned memory design adapts more effectively than human-designed baselines under task distribution shift.
Report issue for preceding element

We evaluate the adaptability of learned memory designs to the distribution shift of tasks.
To evaluate adaptation under distribution shift, memory is collected from 70 tasks in the valid\_seen during the Memory Collection Phase and evaluated on the valid\_unseen using dynamic mode during the Deployment Phase (Appendix [B.4](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A2.SS4 "B.4 Additional Details of Testing ‣ Appendix B Experiment Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")). This tests whether memory designs can adapt to tasks with distribution shift in valid\_unseen by dynamically updating memory.
As shown in [Figure5](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F5 "In 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs"), the learned memory design achieves a success rate of 84.1% on ALFWorld, outperforming all human-designed baselines and demonstrating more effective adaptation under task distribution shift.

Report issue for preceding element

![Refer to caption](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1/x6.png)

Report issue for preceding element

Figure 6: Cost efficiency versus task performance across all benchmarks for different memory designs. The x-axis shows the overall end-to-end memory cost across benchmarks. Bubble size represents the average token size of retrieved content inserted into the agentic system’s context per task. The learned memory design outperforms all baselines while being cost-efficient.
Report issue for preceding element

We measure the cost efficiency of learned memory designs by studying their end-to-end memory cost, defined as the total cost of FMs required to transform raw interaction logs into retrieved content inserted into the agentic system’s context across the Memory Collection and Deployment Phases. We also include the token size of the retrieved content. The details are available in Appendix [A.3](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A1.SS3 "A.3 Evaluation Cost ‣ Appendix A Algorithmic Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs").
As shown in [Figure6](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#S4.F6 "In 4.4 Results ‣ 4 Experiments ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs"), compared to all manual designs, our learned memory designs achieve an average success rate of 53.9%, with lower overall end-to-end memory cost across all benchmarks ($0.09) and appropriate token sizes of retrieved content. The results show ALMA effectively balances memory efficiency and task performance. Since the current algorithm does not explicitly optimize for cost efficiency, we anticipate that incorporating techniques such as multi-objective optimization in future work could enable even more effective exploration of the efficiency-performance Pareto front.

Report issue for preceding element

## 5 Conclusion, Safety, and Future Work

Report issue for preceding element

We propose ALMA, a paradigm that learns to discover new memory designs, enabling agentic systems to continually learn from their past experiences. We evaluate ALMA on a range of sequential decision-making benchmarks and the best-learned designs consistently outperform manually designed baselines, demonstrating the effectiveness of our approach. Furthermore, subsequent experiments show that the learned memory designs exhibit strong scalability, transferability, and cost efficiency. Overall, ALMA provides a principled approach to learn memory design, taking a step toward enabling continual learning in agentic systems.

Report issue for preceding element

ALMA represents a step toward continual learning in AI, contributing to the broader goal of developing AI-generating algorithms beyond current manual approaches (Clune, [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib13 "AI-gas: ai-generating algorithms, an alternate paradigm for producing general artificial intelligence")). However, such AI-generating algorithms introduce unique safety concerns, as components of AI systems are learned rather than designed by humans. The learned components may inadvertently introduce behaviors that deviate from human intentions, ignoring optimization guided by a predefined target metric (Bostrom, [2017](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib68 "Ethical Issues in Advanced Artificial Intelligence"); Rokon et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib67 "SourceFinder: finding malware Source-Code from publicly available repositories in GitHub"); Ecoffet et al., [2020](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib82 "Open questions in creating safe open-ended ai: tensions between control and creativity")).
Recognizing these challenges, we impose explicit constraints and restrictions in our experimental setup during the learning process.
Specifically, each memory design is generated as code by the Meta Agent, they are validated and executed within isolated sandbox environments, with access confined to the sandbox to prevent any interference with the external system, mitigating the risk of unintended actions. We also perform human oversight of learned memory designs to ensure that no potentially harmful actions (e.g., prompt injection) are included in memory designs. In future work, it will be essential to establish a systematic inspection mechanism as the system scales and is deployed, which may include AI and human inspection.

Report issue for preceding element

While ALMA demonstrates strong effectiveness in learning memory designs, it has several limitations that open up future research directions. One limitation is that the memory designs are learned using a pre-defined learning set, instead of dynamically learning memory designs when facing new tasks. Ideally, an adaptive continual learner would be able to learn memory designs online from dynamic data, without separating learning and testing phases. While ALMA has the potential to support online learning, we do not pursue this approach due to computational budget constraints, as evaluating each explored memory design would require a large number of rollouts. Therefore, a possible future direction is to enable online learning of memory design that continually adapts to arbitrary domains.
Another limitation stems from the fact that we demonstrate learning memory designs in code space. While effective, their capabilities may be limited by the underlying FMs.
Future work could explore automated designing and training novel FM architectures with native memory support.

Report issue for preceding element

Overall, ALMA represents a step forward toward automated, continual-learning AI. While current limitations prevent us from demonstrating a system that learns both memory and the agentic system, our results highlight its potential to surpass manually designed memory, showing a way towards “learning to continually learn”.
When developed with the appropriate safety measures, ALMA holds promise for a general-purpose AI capable of learning to continually learn across domains and autonomously adapting to new ones.

Report issue for preceding element

## Impact Statement

Report issue for preceding element

This paper introduces ALMA, a paradigm that automatically learns novel memory designs, thereby enabling agentic systems to continually learn. By leveraging a Meta Agent with open-ended exploration, ALMA mitigates the intensive manual effort conventionally required to handcrafted memory designs for diverse domains. Furthermore, our findings highlight a path toward agentic systems capable of tailoring their memory designs to any context, fostering the development of continual learning of AI. These insights empower practitioners to: (1) automate the development of domain-specific memory designs for specialized fields such as medicine, finance, and software engineering; and (2) uncover memory paradigms that have yet to be identified through human intuition. Ultimately, this work advances the automation of memory design for agentic systems and serves as a foundation for future research on learning memory designs.

Report issue for preceding element

Report issue for preceding elementCode 1: The Python abstract class used in the learning process. In the code, sub-modules are implemented as layers, with each layer encapsulating the functionality of a module. The overall execution order of these update and retrieval operations across sub-modules is orchestrated by the general interfaces: general\_retrieve and general\_update. The example of learned memory design is available at Appendix  [C.3](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#A3.SS3 "C.3 Example Results of Learned Memory Designs ‣ Appendix C Result Details ‣ Learning to Continually Learn via Meta-learning Agentic Memory Designs")

Report issue for preceding element

### A.2 Evaluation of Memory design

Report issue for preceding element

We first formally define a memory design as a triplet ℳ=(U,D,R)\\mathcal{M}=(U,D,R), where UU and RR denote the update and retrieval processes that interface with the agentic system, and DD represents the internal storage structure used for memory retention.
To evaluate a memory design on a dataset 𝒢\\mathcal{G},
we split 𝒢\\mathcal{G} evenly into two subsets:
a set 𝒢collection\\mathcal{G}\_{\\text{collection}} for memory collection and a set 𝒢deployment\\mathcal{G}\_{\\text{deployment}} for evaluation during the Deployment Phase.
During the Memory Collection Phase, the agentic system interacts with the environment to finish tasks in 𝒢collection\\mathcal{G}\_{\\text{collection}} without any memory access,
producing interaction logs that are used to perform memory updates and construct a static memory.
During the Deployment Phase, the agentic system retrieves relevant contexts from the static memory, which are then integrated into the agentic system to facilitate task execution within 𝒢deployment\\mathcal{G}\_{\\text{deployment}}.
The performance on 𝒢deployment\\mathcal{G}\_{\\text{deployment}} reflects the ability of a memory design to collect and utilize knowledge.

Report issue for preceding element

Furthermore, when evaluated on a dataset with task distribution shift during the Deployment Phase, the agentic system first retrieves existing knowledge to construct the knowledge context for the new task, and subsequently updates the memory with the resulting interaction log.

Report issue for preceding element

#### Memory Collection Phase.

Report issue for preceding element

𝒢collection\\mathcal{G}\_{\\text{collection}} is used to collect a set of interaction trajectories
𝒯collection={τcollection(j)}j=1N\\mathcal{T}\_{\\text{collection}}=\\{\\tau^{(j)}\_{\\text{collection}}\\}\_{j=1}^{N}
without any memory access.
Each trajectory τcollection(j)\\tau^{(j)}\_{\\text{collection}} takes the form

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | τcollection(j)=(s1(j),a1(j),s2(j),a2(j),…,snj(j),anj(j)),at(j)∼π(⋅∣s≤t(j),a<t(j))\\tau^{(j)}\_{\\text{collection}}=\\big(s^{(j)}\_{1},a^{(j)}\_{1},s^{(j)}\_{2},a^{(j)}\_{2},\\ldots,s^{(j)}\_{n\_{j}},a^{(j)}\_{n\_{j}}\\big),\\quad a^{(j)}\_{t}\\sim\\pi(\\cdot\\mid s^{(j)}\_{\\leq t},a^{(j)}\_{<t}) |  |

where ss denotes the environment state, and aa denotes the action produced by the fixed agentic policy π\\pi.
For each trajectory, we further obtain a task-level feedback

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | fcollection(j)=F​(τcollection(j))f^{(j)}\_{\\text{collection}}=F\\!\\left(\\tau^{(j)}\_{\\text{collection}}\\right) |  |

where F​(⋅)F(\\cdot) denotes the benchmark-specific evaluation function that assigns a scalar score to each trajectory. We then sequentially update the memory database using the collected interaction logs

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | Dj=U​(τcollection(j),fcollection(j),Dj−1)D\_{j}=U\\!\\left(\\tau^{(j)}\_{\\text{collection}},f^{(j)}\_{\\text{collection}},D\_{j-1}\\right) |  |

where the memory state is updated after each collected trajectory. The final collected memory is denoted as DND\_{N}.

Report issue for preceding element

#### Deployment Phase.

Report issue for preceding element

During the Deployment Phase, we evaluate the performance of the agentic system, with knowledge retrieved from DND\_{N},
on the deployment set 𝒢deployment={gdeployment(i)}i=1K\\mathcal{G}\_{\\text{deployment}}=\\{g^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}}\\}\_{i=1}^{K}.

Report issue for preceding element

For each task gdeployment(i)g^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}}, we first retrieve relevant knowledge from the memory state DND\_{N}

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | e(i)=R​(DN,s1(i))e^{(i)}=R(D\_{N},s^{(i)}\_{1}) |  |

where s1(i)s^{(i)}\_{1} denotes the initial state, including observation and task objective of the current task.
The agent then interacts with the environment, resulting in a trajectory τdeployment(i)\\tau^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}}, where each action is sampled according to

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | at(i)∼π(⋅∣s≤t(i),a<t(i),e(i))a^{(i)}\_{t}\\sim\\pi(\\cdot\\mid s^{(i)}\_{\\leq t},a^{(i)}\_{<t},e^{(i)}) |  |

The feedback is calculated as fdeployment(i)=F​(τdeployment(i))f^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}}=F(\\tau^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}}).
The overall performance of the memory design ℳ\\mathcal{M} is then computed as the average over all deployment tasks

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | fℳ=1K​∑i=1Kfdeployment(i)f\_{\\mathcal{M}}=\\frac{1}{K}\\sum\_{i=1}^{K}f^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}} |  |

When performing Deployment Phase with distribution shift tasks, memory state is further updated sequentially using the collected trajectories τdeployment(i)\\tau^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}}
and feedback signals fdeployment(i)f^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}}, producing DN+iD\_{N+i}.
The updated memory state is then used for retrieval when performing the (i+1)(i+1)-th task.

Report issue for preceding element

### A.3 Evaluation Cost

Report issue for preceding element

#### End-to-End Memory Cost.

Report issue for preceding element

This metric quantifies the cumulative computational overhead incurred by the FMs to generate the content integrated into the agentic system with static mode in Deployment Phase. The cost includes: (1) the expense during the Memory Collection Phase to produce the memory state DND\_{N}; and (2) the cumulative cost of generating knowledge {e(i)}i=1K\\{e^{(i)}\\}\_{i=1}^{K} for all tasks during the Deployment Phase. We suggest that this provides a fair and unbiased way to measure the cost of a memory design, independent of its effectiveness. During the Memory Collection Phase, the agentic system interacts with environments without memory access, ensuring that all trajectories are unaffected by the memory design’s performance. Since these trajectories are the only input used to produce memory, the costs of producing the memory state DND\_{N} and generating relevant knowledge during static deployment depend solely on these trajectories and are therefore independent of the memory design’s effectiveness. We present a summary of the costs associated with testing a memory design across all benchmarks, including GPT-4o-mini and text-embedding-3-small.

Report issue for preceding element

#### Token Size of Retrieved Knowledge.

Report issue for preceding element

We measure the token count of the retrieved context e(i)e^{(i)} for each task during the static Deployment Phase. This metric quantifies the informational overhead injected into the agent’s prompt, serving as a key proxy for measuring the inference-time efficiency of the memory design ℳ\\mathcal{M}. The token size is also independent of effectiveness of a memory design, since it is dependent solely on trajectories collected in Memory Collection Phase. We utilize the GPT-5-mini tokenizer to calculate the token sizes.

Report issue for preceding element

### A.4 Memory Design Archive and Sampling Process

Report issue for preceding element

#### Memory Design Archive.

Report issue for preceding element

To iteratively explore new memory designs, we maintain a memory design archive 𝒜l\\mathcal{A}\_{l} that stores all designs discovered up to learning step ll. For each candidate design ℳ\\mathcal{M}, we perform a stratified sampling process (Thompson, [2012](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib88 "Sampling")) after its evaluation on a large deployment set. Specifically, instead of storing the entire interaction logs, we extract a small subset of size KfK\_{f} as a proxy for the design’s performance. This subset is denoted as

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | 𝒮ℳ={(e(i),τdeployment(i),fdeployment(i))}i=1Kf\\mathcal{S}\_{\\mathcal{M}}=\\{(e^{(i)},\\tau^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}},f\_{\\text{deployment}}^{(i)})\\}\_{i=1}^{K\_{f}} |  |

where each tuple contains the retrieved context e(i)e^{(i)}, the resulting trajectory τdeployment(i)\\tau^{(i)}\_{\\text{deployment}}, and the task-level feedback fdeployment(i)f\_{\\text{deployment}}^{(i)}.

Report issue for preceding element

The archive further records the overall performance fℳf\_{\\mathcal{M}} and the times a memory design is being sampled tℳt\_{\\mathcal{M}}, which tracks the number of times ℳ\\mathcal{M} has been sampled and evaluated across ll learning steps. Formally, at step ll, the memory design archive is defined as the collection of these records

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | 𝒜l={(ℳ,fℳ,𝒮ℳ,tℳ)\|ℳ​ discovered before step ​l}.\\mathcal{A}\_{l}=\\Big\\{(\\mathcal{M},f\_{\\mathcal{M}},\\mathcal{S}\_{\\mathcal{M}},t\_{\\mathcal{M}})\\;\\Big\|\\;\\mathcal{M}\\text{ discovered before step }l\\Big\\}. |  |

#### Sampling Process.

Report issue for preceding element

Similar to prior exploration approaches (Ecoffet et al., [2021](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib76 "First return, then explore"); Zhang et al., [2025e](https://arxiv.org/html/2602.07755v1#bib.bib48 "Darwin godel machine: open-ended evolution of self-improving agents")), given the current memory design archive, we perform a sampling process to sample memory designs that will be used in the next learning step.
For each memory design ℳi\\mathcal{M}\_{i}, we first compute the normalized performance relative to a baseline f0f\_{0}

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | f^ℳi=σ​(fℳi−f0)=11+exp⁡(−λ​(fℳi−f0))\\hat{f}\_{\\mathcal{M}\_{i}}=\\sigma(f\_{\\mathcal{M}\_{i}}-f\_{0})=\\frac{1}{1+\\exp(-\\lambda(f\_{\\mathcal{M}\_{i}}-f\_{0}))} |  |

where f0f\_{0} denotes the performance of the agentic system on 𝒢deployment\\mathcal{G}\_{\\text{deployment}} without memory access.
Subtracting f0f\_{0} ensures that the normalized score reflects the performance gain provided by the memory design.
The final sampling score of each memory design is then given by

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | Ji≡Jℳi=f^ℳi−α​log⁡(1+tℳi)J\_{i}\\equiv J\_{\\mathcal{M}\_{i}}=\\hat{f}\_{\\mathcal{M}\_{i}}-\\alpha\\log(1+t\_{\\mathcal{M}\_{i}}) |  |

where tℳit\_{\\mathcal{M}\_{i}} is the number of times ℳi\\mathcal{M}\_{i} has been sampled, and α\\alpha controls the strength of the penalty.
We then compute the sampling probability using a softmax over the final sampling scores

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | pi=exp⁡(Ji/T−maxj⁡Jj/T)∑j=1\|𝒜l\|exp⁡(Jj/T−maxj⁡Jj/T),i=1,…,\|𝒜l\|p\_{i}=\\frac{\\exp\\big(J\_{i}/T-\\max\_{j}J\_{j}/T\\big)}{\\sum\_{j=1}^{\|\\mathcal{A}\_{l}\|}\\exp\\big(J\_{j}/T-\\max\_{j}J\_{j}/T\\big)},\\quad i=1,\\dots,\|\\mathcal{A}\_{l}\| |  |

where TT is a temperature parameter controlling the smoothness of the distribution. The sampling mechanism prioritizes high-performing yet under-sampled memory designs to balance exploration and exploitation. Meanwhile, designs with moderate performances retain a non-zero sampling probability to maintain diversity throughout the learning process.
Finally, we sample memory designs according to {pi}\\{p\_{i}\\}:

Report issue for preceding element

|     |     |     |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  | ℳsampled⊂{ℳ1,…,ℳ\|𝒜l\|},ℳsampled∼Categorical​(p1,…,p\|𝒜l\|)\\mathcal{M}\_{\\text{sampled}}\\subset\\{\\mathcal{M}\_{1},\\dots,\\mathcal{M}\_{\|\\mathcal{A}\_{l}\|}\\},\\quad\\mathcal{M}\_{\\text{sampled}}\\sim\\text{Categorical}(p\_{1},\\dots,p\_{\|\\mathcal{A}\_{l}\|}) |  |

### A.5 Prompts of Meta Agent

Report issue for preceding element
